<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A high-quality apple genome assembly reveals the association of a retrotransposon and red fruit colour"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2019 10:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-04-02"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="A complete and accurate genome sequence provides a fundamental tool for functional genomics and DNA-informed breeding. Here, we assemble a high-quality genome (contig N50 of 6.99&#8201;Mb) of the apple anther-derived homozygous line HFTH1, including 22 telomere sequences, using a combination of PacBio single-molecule real-time (SMRT) sequencing, chromosome conformation capture (Hi-C) sequencing, and optical mapping. In comparison to the Golden Delicious reference genome, we identify 18,047 deletions, 12,101 insertions and 14 large inversions. We reveal that these extensive genomic variations are largely attributable to activity of transposable elements. Interestingly, we find that a long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposon insertion upstream of MdMYB1, a core transcriptional activator of anthocyanin biosynthesis, is associated with red-skinned phenotype. This finding provides insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying red fruit coloration, and highlights the utility of this high-quality genome assembly in deciphering agriculturally important trait in apple. Existing apple genome assemblies all derive from Golden Delicious. Here, the authors combine different sequencing technologies to assemble a high quality genome of an anther-derived homozygous genotype HFTH1 and find the association of a retrotransposon and red fruit colour."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>A complete and accurate genome sequence provides a fundamental tool for functional genomics and DNA-informed breeding. Here, we assemble a high-quality genome (contig N50 of 6.99âMb) of the apple anther-derived homozygous line HFTH1, including 22 telomere sequences, using a combination of PacBio single-molecule real-time (SMRT) sequencing, chromosome conformation capture (Hi-C) sequencing, and optical mapping. In comparison to the Golden Delicious reference genome, we identify 18,047 deletions, 12,101 insertions and 14 large inversions. We reveal that these extensive genomic variations are largely attributable to activity of transposable elements. Interestingly, we find that a long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposon insertion upstream of <i>MdMYB1</i>, a core transcriptional activator of anthocyanin biosynthesis, is associated with red-skinned phenotype. This finding provides insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying red fruit coloration, and highlights the utility of this high-quality genome assembly in deciphering agriculturally important trait in apple.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The apple genome is a foundation of genetic research and DNA-informed breeding that drives innovations for sustainable apple production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Peace, C. P. DNA-informed breeding of rosaceous crops: promises, progress and prospects. Hortic. Res. 4, 17006 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d25851e630">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Evans, K. The apple genome-harbinger of innovation for sustainable apple production, in Achieving Sustainable Cultivation of Apples (Burleigh Dodds Science Publishing Limited, Cambridge, 2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d25851e633">2</a></sup>. Although the availability of the current high-quality genome of Golden Delicious and the resequencing of major genotypes enable rapid progress in apple genomics and breeding studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Daccord, N. et al. High-quality de novo assembly of the apple genome and methylome dynamics of early fruit development. Nat. Genet. 49, 1099â1106 (2017)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d25851e637">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus Ã domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833â839 (2010)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d25851e637_1">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, X. et al. Improved hybrid de novo genome assembly of domesticated apple (MalusÃdomestica). GigaScience 5, 35 (2016)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d25851e637_2">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Duan, N. et al. Genome re-sequencing reveals the history of apple and supports a two-stage model for fruit enlargement. Nat. Commun. 8, 249 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d25851e640">6</a></sup>, only a single reference genome together with short-read resequencing data presents some limitations in the discovery of new genes and characterisation of genomic variations, which may substantially contribute to genome evolution and the genetics of complex traits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Daccord, N. et al. High-quality de novo assembly of the apple genome and methylome dynamics of early fruit development. Nat. Genet. 49, 1099â1106 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d25851e644">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Lisch, D. How important are transposons for plant evolution? Nat. Rev. Genet. 14, 49â61 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d25851e647">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Espley, R. V. et al. Multiple repeats of a promoter segment causes transcription factor autoregulation in red apples. Plant Cell 21, 168â183 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d25851e650">8</a></sup>. A large-scale survey of genomic variations will provide insights into the potential biological mechanisms of key traits, which in turn will aid the development of genetic markers for marker-assisted selection (MAS) breeding in apple. Recent studies have demonstrated that an extensive range of functional genomic variation can be readily uncovered through direct comparative analyses of the several high-quality genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Zhang, J. et al. Extensive sequence divergence between the reference genomes of two elite indica rice varieties Zhenshan 97 and Minghui 63. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. 113, E5163âE5171 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d25851e654">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Chakraborty, M. et al. Hidden genetic variation shapes the structure of functional elements in Drosophila. Nat. Genet. 50, 20 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d25851e657">10</a></sup>. In addition, apple (<i>Malus domestica</i> Borkh.) is among the most diverse and economically important fruit species in the Rosaceae family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Daccord, N. et al. High-quality de novo assembly of the apple genome and methylome dynamics of early fruit development. Nat. Genet. 49, 1099â1106 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d25851e665">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Duan, N. et al. Genome re-sequencing reveals the history of apple and supports a two-stage model for fruit enlargement. Nat. Commun. 8, 249 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d25851e668">6</a></sup>, but we still lack an in-depth understanding of genetic basis of its many economically important traits. In the case of red skin colouration, although the developmental and environmental regulatory mechanisms of the anthocyanin biosynthetic pathway have been well characterised and the corresponding genes have been identified<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Takos, A. M. et al. Light-induced expression of a MYB gene regulates anthocyanin biosynthesis in red apples. Plant Physiol. 142, 1216â1232 (2006)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d25851e672">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Telias, A. et al. Apple skin patterning is associated with differential expression of MYB10. BMC Plant Biol. 11, 93 (2011)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d25851e672_1">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ban, Y. et al. Isolation and functional analysis of a MYB transcription factor gene that is a key regulator for the development of red coloration in apple skin. Plant Cell Physiol. 48, 958â970 (2007)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d25851e672_2">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Jaakola, L. New insights into the regulation of anthocyanin biosynthesis in fruits. Trends Plant. Sci. 18, 477â483 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d25851e675">14</a></sup>, the genetic basis for the regulation of fruits colouration is not yet fully understood. Dissection of the genetic determinants of this crucial trait appears to be difficult without the availability of high-quality genome sequences.</p><p>All cultivated apple genotypes are a highly heterozygous and ancient autotetraploid of 17 chromosomes, presenting enormous challenges for genome analyses and breeding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Daccord, N. et al. High-quality de novo assembly of the apple genome and methylome dynamics of early fruit development. Nat. Genet. 49, 1099â1106 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d25851e682">3</a></sup>. Thus, the anther-derived homozygous genotype HFTH1 was developed for sequencing (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig1">1a</a>). This homozygous line has the advantage of simplifying genome assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Daccord, N. et al. High-quality de novo assembly of the apple genome and methylome dynamics of early fruit development. Nat. Genet. 49, 1099â1106 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d25851e689">3</a></sup>, and its parentage Hanfu (DongguangâxâFuji) is a very influential cultivar in China, that has several desirable traits, including bright red skin, cold-resistance and long shelf-life<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Zhao, D. et al. Evaluation of the specific characters of Hanfu apple cultivar. J. Fruit. Sci. 26, 6â12 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d25851e693">15</a></sup>. Here, we present a high-quality reference genome of HFTH1 using a combination of SMRT sequencing, Hi-C sequencing and optical mapping. We then use this genome to perform a comparative genomic analysis with the existing apple genomes. We track the highly dynamic evolution of transposons, and discover an LTR retrotransposon that is associated with the red-skinned phenotype and thus serves as a valuable tool for MAS breeding. This additional reference genome provides a foundation for functional genomics and transposon biology, and enhances our understanding of the genome variation that shapes phenotypic diversity in apple.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09518-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9518_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09518-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9518_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="853" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Overview of the assembly quality and characteristics of the HFTH1 genome. <b>a</b> Regenerated plantlets derived from Hanfu anther culture and the dramatic changes between the HFTH1 phenotype and the heterozygous donor (HFP) genotype. <b>b</b> Homozygosity analysis of chromosome 01 of HFTH1 and HFP using simple sequence repeats (SSRs, the results of the analysis of all chromosomes are shown in Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">1</a>). <b>c</b> Counts of SNPs detected in the GDDH13 and HFTH1 genomes. SNPs were detected using Illumina reads from the same individual that was used for the assembly. The heterozygous SNP (red) represents the heterozygosity of a genome and the homozygous SNP (blue) represents the potential error for an assembly. <b>d</b> Distribution of the contig length in the assemblies of <i>Arabidopsis</i> (TAIR10), Nipponbare (RGAP7), HFTH1 and GDDH13. Each contig size (<i>y</i>-axis) represents the minimum contig size that covered the cumulative percentage of the assembly (<i>x</i>-axis) after the assembled contigs were sorted from the largest to the smallest. <b>e</b> HiâC interactions among 17 chromosomes with a 100-kb resolution. Strong interactions are indicated in dark red and weak interactions are indicated in yellow. <b>f</b> Circular diagram depicting the characteristics of the HFTH1 genome. The tracks from outer to inner circles indicate the following: <b>a</b> chromosomes (Chr.), gaps and telomeres, the black regions at the end of each chromosome represent assembled the telomere sequences and the grey regions represent gap regions; <b>b</b> gene density (window size of 1âMb); <b>c</b> repeat density (window size of 1âMb); <b>d</b> Copia-7; <b>e</b> Golden Delicious shared SNPs (window size of 500âkb)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>To minimise the complexity of assembly, an anther-derived trihaploid Hanfu line (HFTH1) was used for genome sequencing, while its donor Hanfu was a highly heterozygous diploid cultivar (HFP, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig1">1a, b</a> and Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">1</a>). The homozygosity of the HFTH1 genome was confirmed by microsatellite markers and k-mer spectrum analysis (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig1">1b</a> and Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">2</a>). The HFTH1 genome was determined as a triploidy by flow cytometry analysis, suggesting that HFTH1 had undergone spontaneous chromosome duplication during in vitro culture (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">3</a>). Compared with the recently published genome of double haploid Golden Delicious line<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Daccord, N. et al. High-quality de novo assembly of the apple genome and methylome dynamics of early fruit development. Nat. Genet. 49, 1099â1106 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d25851e793">3</a></sup> (GDDH13), HFTH1 was more homozygous, as demonstrated by calling heterozygous SNPs from Illumina reads obtained from the corresponding individual (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig1">1c</a>). This higher level of homozygosity is more favourable for improving the quality of the genome assembly.</p><p>Our sequencing of HFTH1 resulted in coverage of ~117-fold PacBio single-molecule long reads (77âGb with an average length of 13.1âkb), 66-fold Illumina paired-end short reads (43.3âGb), 224-fold optical map data (147.8âGb with an average length of 178.9âkb) and 145-fold Hi-C data (95.5âGb, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">1</a>). The assembly was performed in a stepwise fashion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Bickhart, D. M. et al. Single-molecule sequencing and chromatin conformation capture enable de novo reference assembly of the domestic goat genome. Nat. Genet. 49, 643â650 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d25851e806">16</a></sup>, and the initial assembly of the PacBio-only data generated a 656.52âMb genome size with a contig N50 of 4.63âMb (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">2</a>). The initial contigs were polished with PacBio long reads and Illumina short reads. Subsequently, the polished contigs were scaffolded using optical map data, and during this step four contigs containing conflicting connections were identified and split to resolve conflicts, and 58.5% gaps that were introduced in this step were closed by subsequent gap filling procedure. Finally, scaffolding with Hi-C data allowed the accurate clustering and ordering of 17 pseudo-chromosomes covering the 658.90âMb assembly, with a contig N50 of 6.99âMb and a maximum contig length of 18.01âMb (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">3</a> and Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig1">1d, e</a>). The assembly size was close to the estimated genome size of GDDH13<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Daccord, N. et al. High-quality de novo assembly of the apple genome and methylome dynamics of early fruit development. Nat. Genet. 49, 1099â1106 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d25851e820">3</a></sup>, but represented 92.99% of our estimated genome size (708.54âMb) for HFTH1 by k-mer analysis, and ~97.89% of the Illumina reads of HFTH1 could be mapped to our assembly (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">4</a>). In addition, the 160,068âbp chloroplast genome and 396,939âbp mitochondria genome were assembled into two complete contigs (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Assessment of genome quality</h3><p>The quality and completeness of the assembly were evaluated using several different strategies. For the base accuracy of the sequencing, the quality value (QV) of the assembly was estimated to be at least 41, which compared very favourably with those of two published mammalian genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Bickhart, D. M. et al. Single-molecule sequencing and chromatin conformation capture enable de novo reference assembly of the domestic goat genome. Nat. Genet. 49, 643â650 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d25851e838">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Gordon, D. et al. Long-read sequence assembly of the gorilla genome. Science 352, aae0344âaae0344 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d25851e841">17</a></sup> (QV35 for gorilla and QV34.5 for goat) that were also assembled with PacBio data. For the structural accuracy of assembly, ~98.71% of the mapped Illumina reads of HFTH1 could be mapped with the correct orientation and estimated insert size, versus 94.36% of the mapped reads of GDDH13 (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">4</a>). Furthermore, the whole-genome alignment of HFTH1 and GDDH13 showed strong collinearity and consistency (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">5</a>).</p><p>Our assembly captured 22 long stretches of telomeric sequences (5â²-TTTAGGG-3â²) at both ends of seven chromosomes and at a single end of eight chromosomes, with repeat numbers ranging from 294 to 1073 (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Tab1">1</a> and Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig1">1f</a>). Moreover, based on a benchmark of 1440 conserved plant genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d25851e860">18</a></sup>, ~97.0% complete BUSCO genes and 98.02% of the expressed sequence tags (ESTs) of <i>Malus domestica</i> from GenBank could be detected in the assembly (Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">5</a>), confirming the high completeness of the assembly. In addition, the contig N50 value of our assembly was comparable to that of the rice genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Kawahara, Y. et al. Improvement of the Oryza sativa Nipponbare reference genome using next generation sequence and optical map data. Rice 6, 4 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d25851e874">19</a></sup> (RGAP7), and the HFTH1 genome covered approximately the same BUSCOs as the <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Berardini, T. Z. et al. The Arabidopsis information resource: making and mining the âgold standardâ annotated reference plant genome. Genesis 53, 474â485 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d25851e881">20</a></sup> (TAIR10) and RGAP7 genome (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">3</a> and Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig1">1d</a>), which demonstrated that the level of completeness of our genome was at a similar to that of the model plant genomes, even though HFTH1 had the largest genome size and the highest proportion of repetitive sequences compared with the genomes of <i>Arabidopsis</i> and rice (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">3</a>). The completeness of our genome provided an opportunity to comprehensively assess genome variations between HFTH1 and GDDH13.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of HFTH1 and GDDH13 genome assemblies</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome annotation</h3><p>We identified 44,677 high-confidence protein-coding genes, and ~4.29% of annotated genes were located in the gap regions of the GDDH13 genome. The annotated genes covered 95.9% of the complete BUSCO genes (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">3</a>), and ~92.28% of the annotated genes were expressed in at least one tissue or homologous to known proteins, which suggested that our genes annotation was very complete.</p><p>We employed a combination of de novo and homology-based approaches to annotate repetitive sequences. Approximately 393.88âMb and 362.18âMb transposable elements (TEs) were identified in the HFTH1 and GDDH13 genomes, respectively (Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">6</a>). The difference in the repeat sequence content (~32âMb) can account for 93.09% of the additional non-N bases between the HFTH1 and GDDH13 genomes. Although we were unable to identify any significantly enriched tandem centromeric repeat elements in the HFTH1 genome using existing approaches<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Hibrand Saint-Oyant, L. et al. A high-quality genome sequence of Rosa chinensis to elucidate ornamental traits. Nat. Plants 4, 473â484 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d25851e1799">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Melters, D. P. et al. Comparative analysis of tandem repeats from hundreds of species reveals unique insights into centromere evolution. Genome Biol. 14, R10 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d25851e1802">22</a></sup>, 17 blocks (one block per chromosome) with particularly high proportions of repeat elements (â&gt;90%) in the HFTH1 genome, were located near the middle of chromosomes (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">7</a> and Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig1">1f</a>). These regions can be assumed to represent part of putative heterochromatin regions on the HFTH1 chromosomes. The most abundant repeat element family in these regions was Copia-7 (ID from Repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462â467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d25851e1813">23</a></sup>), and most of the Copia-7 elements were concentrated in these regions (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig1">1f</a>). Although <i>HODOR</i> (a high-copy Golden Delicious repeat) was identified as the most repetitive consensus sequence in the apple genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Daccord, N. et al. High-quality de novo assembly of the apple genome and methylome dynamics of early fruit development. Nat. Genet. 49, 1099â1106 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d25851e1823">3</a></sup>, the Copia-7 elements showed low similarity with <i>HODOR</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Gap filling for the reference Golden Delicious genome</h3><p>Owing to the genomic congruence between GDDH13 and HFTH1, we used the HFTH1 genome to fill gaps in the GDDH13 genome, as a similar approach has been applied to the genomes of human<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Shi, L. et al. Long-read sequencing and de novo assembly of a Chinese genome. Nat. Commun. 7, 12065 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d25851e1838">24</a></sup>, gorilla<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Gordon, D. et al. Long-read sequence assembly of the gorilla genome. Science 352, aae0344âaae0344 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d25851e1842">17</a></sup> and goat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Bickhart, D. M. et al. Single-molecule sequencing and chromatin conformation capture enable de novo reference assembly of the domestic goat genome. Nat. Genet. 49, 643â650 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d25851e1846">16</a></sup>. In total, 488 gaps (adjacent gaps were merged and counted as one gap) in the GDDH13 genome, with average and median lengths of 78,864âbp and 15,647âbp, were completely closed (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">6a</a>). Among these, the longest gap closure was 2,859,572âbp and spanned 256 genes with 33.98% tandem duplicated genes. Approximately 97.75% of the closed gaps were located in repeat regions (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">6b</a>), and most of these gaps had been assembled into multiple short fragments or filled with ambiguous (N) bases in previously published genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Daccord, N. et al. High-quality de novo assembly of the apple genome and methylome dynamics of early fruit development. Nat. Genet. 49, 1099â1106 (2017)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d25851e1857">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus Ã domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833â839 (2010)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d25851e1857_1">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Li, X. et al. Improved hybrid de novo genome assembly of domesticated apple (MalusÃdomestica). GigaScience 5, 35 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d25851e1860">5</a></sup>. In particular, ~39.34% sequences of the filled genomic gaps in GDDH13 could be found on its unanchored Chr00. For example, one 719,872âbp gap (the expected gap size was 728,347âbp) could be completely closed using the HFTH1 assembly, whereas this gap was assembled into 2 and 11 fragments in the assemblies reported by Velasco et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus Ã domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833â839 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d25851e1864">4</a></sup> and Li et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Li, X. et al. Improved hybrid de novo genome assembly of domesticated apple (MalusÃdomestica). GigaScience 5, 35 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d25851e1868">5</a></sup>, respectively (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">6c</a>). In addition, we filled nine genomic gaps with an average length of 42,360âbp for the HFTH1 genome using the GDDH13 genome. These additional sequences will help to improve genomic annotation and discover thousands of functional genes and regulatory elements.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genome comparison between Golden Delicious and Hanfu</h3><p>Genome variations, such as insertions, deletions, inversions and duplications, are an important source of the genetic diversity that shapes phenotypic variations. Hanfu exhibits an obviously different phenotype from that of Golden Delicious, including red skin, a strong cold-resistant habit, a high resistance to alternaria leaf spot and branch ring-rot, long fruit storability, and an obvious short branching character<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Zhao, D. et al. Evaluation of the specific characters of Hanfu apple cultivar. J. Fruit. Sci. 26, 6â12 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d25851e1884">15</a></sup>. The comparison of the Golden Delicious genome with our HFTH1 reference genome, allowed us to directly catalogue the extent of the genomic variation between these two cultivars.</p><p>First, an average density of 2.15 Golden Delicious shared SNPs per kilobase was identified (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig1">1f</a>). Approximately 3.34% of the SNPs were located within 31.69% of protein-coding genes containing non-synonymous substitutions. An enrichment analysis of the InterPro domains of genes with non-synonymous SNPs, showed that these genes were significantly correlated with the disease-resistant domains (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">8</a>), which demonstrated that these genes may evolve under different selection pressures in two cultivars, and provide resistance to various environmental stresses.</p><p>We identified 18,047 deletions and 12,101 insertions (including absence/presence variations) that were had a length greater than 100âbp in length. Of these, the presences of 63.73% of the deletions and 54.46% of the insertions in all published genomes of Golden Delicious (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig2">2a</a>), were defined as Golden Delicious shared structural variations (GDSVs). An enrichment analysis of the genes associated with GDSVs agreed with the results of the non-synonymous SNP analysis (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">9</a>). For instance, one GDSV that includes a short interspersed nuclear element (SINE) was inserted in the 3â²UTR of a disease-resistance gene (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">7a</a>). Another GDSV was a deletion in the 5â²UTR of a C-repeat/DRE binding factor (<i>MdCBF2</i>), which is a master regulator of cold acclimation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Miura, K. &amp; Furumoto, T. Cold signaling and cold response in plants. Int. J. Mol. Sci. 14, 5312â5337 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d25851e1912">25</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">7b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09518-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9518_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09518-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9518_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="682" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Characterisation of structural variants. <b>a</b> The overlap of structural variations (â&gt;â100âbp) between GDDH13 and other published assemblies of Golden Delicious. <b>b</b> Classification of repeat elements associated with structural variations. A repeat element was defined as being associated with a structural variation if it overlaps with the structural variation. LTR, long terminal repeat; DNA, DNA transposon; SINE, short interspersed element; LINE, long interspersed element; Other, other types of repeat; Non, no repeats were detected. <b>c</b> Divergence rate of transposons of different sources. Deletion and insertion represent transposons associated with deletions and insertions, respectively. RepeatMasker was used to calculate the divergence rate from the consensus sequence of Repbase for each transposon. The middle hinge of all boxes is the median, the lower and upper hinges correspond to the 25th and 75th percentiles, and the whiskers represent the 1.5 inter-quartile range (IQR) extending from the hinges. Wilcoxon rank sum test, ***<i>p</i>â&lt;â0.001 and <i>n</i>â=â435,202, 456,919, 6525, 4010 for GDDH13, HFTH1, Deletion, Insertion, respectively. <b>d</b> Count of structural variations detected in each chromosome. Duplicated chromosomes are shown in the <i>x</i>-axis with the same colour. <b>e</b> Synteny view of two large inversions with a length longer than 600âbp in chromosome 16 (19,782,372â20,450,041 and 31,388,523â32,032,862). Source data are provided in Source Data file 1</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In addition, among the GDSVs, the average lengths of the deletions (508âbp) and insertions (519âbp) were very similar (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">8</a>), and &gt;70% of GDSVs were associated with TEs. The TE distribution patterns of insertions and deletions were similar, with the exception that the percentage of SINEs associated with insertion was approximately twofold higher than that found for deletions, which accounted for only 3â8% of GDSVs (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig2">2b</a>). We found that the divergence rates of TEs displayed a significant difference (<i>p</i>-valueâ&lt;â2.2e-16; Welch two-sample <i>t</i>-test), and that TEs associated with insertions were less divergent from the consensus TEs found in Repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462â467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d25851e1978">23</a></sup> and younger than the TEs associated with deletions (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig2">2c</a>), which suggests that most of the Golden Delicious shared insertions were induced by young TEs insertion in the HFTH1 genome, after Hanfu and Golden Delicious diverged from a common ancestor.</p><p>Taking a whole-genome view of the distribution of SNPs and GDSVs, we found chr.15 had the highest average SNP density (2.99 per kilobase, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig1">1f</a>), whereas chr.2 exhibited the most structural variations (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig2">2d</a>). Most of the duplicated chromosomes from the same chromosome ancestor after a recent whole-genome duplication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus Ã domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833â839 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d25851e1996">4</a></sup> (WGD, chrs.3 and 11, 9 and 17, 13 and 16) showed similar contents of SNPs and GDSVs, whereas chrs.10 and 5 showed significantly different contents of SNPs (2.52:1, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig1">1f</a>) and GDSVs (1.66:1, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig2">2d</a>), particularly in one end of chr.5, which showed a lower diversity than the average diversity of whole-genome. However, chrs.10 and 5 had a similar gene content (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig1">1f</a>), suggesting that chr.5 may have undergone introgression and fixation during domestication and breeding process after the recent WGD.</p><p>Additionally, we identified 14 large inversions (INVs) with length longer than 100âkb, two of which on chr.16 were longer than 600âkb (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig2">2e</a>). We found that all the breakpoints of these two INVs were located within TE elements, which may have induced these inversion events.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Dynamic evolution of LTR retrotransposons in Hanfu</h3><p>TEs play an important role in driving adaptive evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Lisch, D. How important are transposons for plant evolution? Nat. Rev. Genet. 14, 49â61 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2024">7</a></sup>, and &gt;75% of the TEs from the apple genome were LTR-RTs. To track the highly dynamic evolution of LTR-RTs, we identified 7313 intact LTR-RTs with an average length of 7868âbp (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig3">3a</a>) in the HFTH1 genome. Approximately 62% of these LTR-RTs were present in the Golden Delicious genome based on its different assemblies (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig3">3b</a> and Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">9</a>), suggesting that these LTR-RTs may have been inserted in the common ancestor of the Hanfu and Golden Delicious lines. In the Rosaceae family, pear and apple share a highly conserved genomes. However, because of the fragmented genome assembly of the pear genome (contig N50 of 33.76âkb), only 40 intact LTR-RTs were found in the pear genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="ChagnÃ©, D. et al. The draft genome sequence of European pear (Pyrus communis L.âBartlettâ). PLoS ONE 9, e92644 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2037">26</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">9</a>). Further analysis showed that most existing intact LTR-RTs in the apple genome may have been inserted after the divergence of apple and pear, which is in accordance with the findings that the average insert time (0.8 MYA) of these LTR-RTs was notably less than the estimated divergence time (8.1 MYA) of apple and pear (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig3">3c</a> and Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">10</a>). More than half of the shared LTR-RTs were highly similar between the HFTH1 and GDDH13 genomes (identityââ¥0.99, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig3">3b</a>), and the average cumulative nucleotide substitutions (CNS) of the shared LTR-RTs and their flanking regions was less than the average level across the whole-genome (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig3">3d</a>) and most of the reverse-transcriptase domains (<i>gag, pol</i>, and <i>env</i>) of the shared LTR-RTs were not expressed in the ten tested samples (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">11a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09518-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9518_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09518-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9518_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="764" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Evolution of intact LTR retrotransposons in the HFTH1 genome. <b>a</b> Length distribution of intact LTR-RTs from the HFTH1 genome. A large number of LTR-RTs in the GDDH13 genome have a length &lt;100âbp, which indicated that these LTR-RTs were newly inserted into the HFTH1 genome or eliminated from the GDDH13 genome. <b>b</b> Pie chart showing the classification of the LTR-RTs in the HFTH1 genome (see Methods for the definition of each type). <b>c</b> Estimated divergence time of each category of LTR-RTs in the HFTH1 genome. The divergence time was calculated using a substitution rate of 1.3âÃâ10<sup>â8</sup> substitutions per site per year. Wilcoxon rank sum test, ***<i>p</i>â&lt;â0.001 and <i>n</i>â=â3468, 1850, 5800 for Shared, Insertion, Total, respectively. <b>d</b> Distribution of cumulative nucleotide substitutions of LTR-RTs and flanking sequences. The nucleotide substitutions of both flanking regions were calculated in a 400âbp sliding window, and the nucleotide substitutions of LTR-RT inside regions were calculated in ten non-overlapping equal bins. Three thousand blocks in the HFTH1 genome with a length 20âkb were randomly selected and used as a control. <b>e</b> Distribution of insert sites of specific LTR-RTs in the GDDH13 genome in 100-bp windows located at an increasing distance from genes. The distribution of intergenic distance was used as a control. <b>f</b> Distribution of cumulative nucleotide substitutions of flanking sequences over the divergence time of LTR-RTs. The flanking sequences were defined as 500âbp fragments located the upstream and downstream of LTR-RTs. The cumulative nucleotide substitutions were calculated in a sliding window of 100,000 years. <b>g</b> Rate of cumulative nucleotide substitutions of flanking sequences relative to the divergence time of LTR-RTs (see Methods for details). The middle hinge of all boxes is the median, the lower and upper hinges correspond to the 25th and 75th percentiles, and the whiskers represent the 1.5 inter-quartile range (IQR) extending from the hinges. Source data of Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM7">3</a>a, b, c and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM7">e</a> are provided in Source Data file 1. Source data of Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM8">3</a>d, f and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM8">g</a> are provided in Source Data file 2</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In addition, specific LTR-RTs (~31.9% of the total LTR-RTs) with two short target site duplications (TSDs) on their both sides, were only found on the HFTH1 genome (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig3">3b</a>), but only one of two TSDs was found in the corresponding position of the GDDH13 genome, suggesting that these specific LTR-RTs were inserted after the divergence of Hanfu and Golden Delicious. These specific insertion events, especially those near or inside genes, may be under strong selection for fixing, because protein-coding genes (coding plus intron regions) can account for 22.22% of whole-genome, but only 12.05% of the insertion events were located inside genes. The observed insertion events near genes were also lower than expected (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig3">3e</a>). Besides, the average expression level of genes near these insertion sites was less than that of the total gene set (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">11b</a>). However, the selection pressure may be weakened or lost when the insertions located at least 1âkb from a gene (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig3">3e</a>). We found that 82.54% of specific LTR-RTs were expressed at least in one tested samples, compared with 64.39% of shared LTR-RTs (<i>p</i>-valueâ&lt;â2.2e-16; chi-squared test), which indicated that most specific LTR-RTs were young and active. The insertion events not only affect nearby gene expression, but also increase the mutation rate in the vicinity of the insertion site (~1.4- to 1.8-fold higher than the rate of the global nucleotide substitutions), which was in agreement with prior investigations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Hollister, J. D., Ross-Ibarra, J. &amp; Gaut, B. S. Indel-associated mutation rate varies with mating system in flowering plants. Mol. Biol. Evol. 27, 409â416 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2147">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Tian, D. et al. Single-nucleotide mutation rate increases close to insertions/deletions in eukaryotes. Nature 455, 105â108 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2150">28</a></sup>. Furthermore, the data also showed that the CNSs decreased as the distance from an LTR-RT increased; whereas the average CNSs in sequences surrounding shared inactive LTR-RTs was substantially lower (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig3">3d</a>). We found that the average CNSs increased gradually as the divergence time of LTR-RTs increased (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig3">3f</a>). Furthermore, the average CNSs rate appeared to slow and reach a bottleneck as the transposons were gradually inactivated and became other non-functional sequences (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig3">3g</a>). This result was consistent with the finding that most of the CNSs in the shared LTR-RTs were less than those of the specific LTR-RTs (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig3">3d</a>) even though the shared LTR-RTs had a greater evolutionary age (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig3">3c</a>). In addition, we found that ~2.9% of the LTR-RTs in the HFTH1 genome may have been eliminated in the GDDH13 genome, because 66.27% of these LTR-RTs had a length shorter than 100âbp at the corresponding site in the GDDH13 genome. In addition, the others had no blast hits (BLASTN <i>e</i>-valuesââ¤â10) to the corresponding LTR-RT sequence in the HFTH1 genome, and most of these sequences appear to be the remains of the transposition occurrence of other TEs in the GDDH13 genome (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig3">3b</a>). The average CNSs of the sequences surrounding these eliminated LTR-RTs was the highest in the genome (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig3">3d</a>), suggesting elimination events might have made a greater impact than insertions did during the evolution of TEs. The dynamic evolution of TEs likely created the unique genetic and phenotypic characteristics of HFTH1.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Retrotransposon as an enhancer of <i>MdMYB1</i> expression</h3><p>To evaluate the potential of our genome assembly for the genetic dissection of agriculturally important traits, we focused on red phenotype of apple fruit, which is a key determinant of consumer preference<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Telias, A. et al. Apple skin patterning is associated with differential expression of MYB10. BMC Plant Biol. 11, 93 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2191">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ban, Y. et al. Isolation and functional analysis of a MYB transcription factor gene that is a key regulator for the development of red coloration in apple skin. Plant Cell Physiol. 48, 958â970 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2194">13</a></sup>. Genetic evidence has confirmed that <i>MdMYB1</i>, a core transcriptional regulator of the anthocyanin biosynthesis pathway, is responsible for the red phenotype<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Takos, A. M. et al. Light-induced expression of a MYB gene regulates anthocyanin biosynthesis in red apples. Plant Physiol. 142, 1216â1232 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2201">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ban, Y. et al. Isolation and functional analysis of a MYB transcription factor gene that is a key regulator for the development of red coloration in apple skin. Plant Cell Physiol. 48, 958â970 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2204">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Espley, R. V. et al. Red colouration in apple fruit is due to the activity of the MYB transcription factor, MdMYB10. Plant J. 49, 414â427 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2207">29</a></sup>. In this study, we detected a significant difference between the transcript levels of <i>MdMYB1</i> and anthocyanin-related structural genes in the skins of ripening fruits of Hanfu and Golden Delicious by quantitative reverse transcription PCR (qRT-PCR) (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">12</a>). <i>MdMYB1</i> had at least three types of alleles, namely, <i>MdMYB1-1</i>, <i>MdMYB1-2</i> and <i>MdMYB1-3</i>, and <i>MdMYB1-1</i> was a single dominant allele controlling anthocyanin synthesis in apple skin. The <i>MdMYB1-2</i> and <i>MdMYB1-3</i> alleles in non-red-skinned cultivars show a limited expression under intense light and low-temperature<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Telias, A. et al. Apple skin patterning is associated with differential expression of MYB10. BMC Plant Biol. 11, 93 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2240">12</a></sup>. Furthermore, it has been shown that the coding region differences of these alleles do not affect their functional activity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ban, Y. et al. Isolation and functional analysis of a MYB transcription factor gene that is a key regulator for the development of red coloration in apple skin. Plant Cell Physiol. 48, 958â970 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2244">13</a></sup>. The reason for the significant differences in the expression levels among <i>MdMYB1</i> alleles has not been fully elucidated.</p><p>The availability of two high-quality genomes allows a more precise comparative analysis. Specifically, we performed a sequence alignment of <i>MdMYB1</i> in the HFTH1 and GDDH13 genomes. The results showed that the coding sequences of <i>MdMYB1</i> were identical, but one SNP was detected in the intron regions. In addition, fifteen SNPs and five indels in the upstream region were found. Among of five indels, one 501âbp insertion occurs in the GDDH13 at a distance of â3394âbp upstream of the ATG initiation codon of <i>MdMYB1</i>, and is highly divergent from the neighbouring LTR-RTs (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig4">4a</a>); Another 4097âbp insertion is present in the HFTH1 at a distance of â3297âbp upstream of the ATG initiation codon of <i>MdMYB1</i>, and is a gypsy-like LTR retrotransposon (denoted redTE) with two TSDs (CATAT, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig4">4a</a>). Its two flanking LTR sequences (1274âbp) were completely identical, indicating that it was a recent insertion event. Although at least 3913 intact Gypsy-like retrotransposons were identified in the HFTH1 genome, only one of them had a 96.26% global identity with redTE (redTE-like, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">13</a>), and the others showed &lt;75.10% identity. Interestingly, redTE-like was only found in the HFTH1 genome, and not in GDDH13 through a genome-wide scan, and its two flanking LTR sequences (1262âbp and 1298âbp) harboured more mutations, suggesting that redTE-like was older than redTE.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09518-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9518_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09518-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9518_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="905" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Red phenotype of apple associated with an LTR retrotransposon. <b>a</b> Molecular structure of <i>MdMYB1-1</i> and <i>MdMYB1-2</i> alleles with flanking sequences. The insertion sites upstream of <i>MdMYB1-1</i> and <i>MdMYB1-2</i> are indicated by a red line (HFTH1) and golden yellow line (GDDH13), respectively. <b>b</b> Images of 12 well-known apple varieties with non-red or red skin colour (upper panel) and PCR-based screen showing the absence (right) or presence (left) of the LTR retrotransposon insertion in the upstream of <i>MdMYB1</i>. A 750âbp fragment corresponding to the partial of redTE that is absent in non-red-skinned varieties (lanes 1 to 6) and is present only in red-skinned varieties (lanes 7 to 12). Lane 13, control check (purified water was used as the template), Lane 14, positive control. <b>c</b> PCR-based analysis of the redTE insertion in 145 accessions and the F1 segregating population from the cross of HuayueâÃâHoneycrisp. <b>d</b> Constructs and transient expression assays showing that redTE obviously enhanced the luciferase expression levels. Upper panel, the mpCaM:<i>luc</i> (up) and redTE:mpCaM:<i>luc</i> (down) construct backbone consists of the minimal promoter from the cauliflower mosaic virus (mpCaMV, grey box), luciferase ORF and cauliflower mosaic virus terminator (black box). Lower panel, Luciferase image of <i>Nicotiana benthamiana</i> leaves 72âh after infiltration with the Agrobacterial strains containing mpCaM:<i>luc</i> (left), and redTE:mpCaM:<i>luc</i> (right), respectively. <b>e</b> MdCBF2 binds directly to the cis-acting element GCCGACTT. Source data of Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig4">4</a>b, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig4">d</a> are provided in Source Data file 1</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To determine whether these differences were associated with fruits skin colour, we retrieved the sequences of these differential locis using data from public databases (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">10</a>). The results showed that sixteen SNPs, two small indels in HFTH1 were also present in non-red-skinned accessions, and one 2-bp indel located in 17 repeat T bases in HFTH1 have a complex polymorphism in red apple accessions. In addition, the 501âbp insertion in the GDDH13 genome was also found in red-skinned accessions by PCR verification (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">14</a>). Subsequently, we investigated the association of redTE with red skin colour, because it has been reported that retrotransposons often play crucial roles in tissue-specific expression patterns of pigment genes in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Butelli, E. et al. Retrotransposons control fruit-specific, cold-dependent accumulation of anthocyanins in blood oranges. Plant Cell 24, 1242â1255 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2356">30</a></sup> and animals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="vanât Hof, A. E. et al. The industrial melanism mutation in British peppered moths is a transposable element. Nature 534, 102 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2360">31</a></sup>. First, we retrieved resequencing data of the cultivars from GDR (ftp.bioinfo.wsu.edu, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">11</a>) using the junction sequence GGATTTTTATATATGTGTTGACCCTA of redTE. The results showed that this target junction sequence were able to be found from the data of only red-skinned cultivars. Next, we screened 112 red-skinned accessions and 33 non-red-skinned accessions with known phenotypes using a PCR marker specific to redTE. The results showed that all the tested red-skinned accessions were completely associated with redTE, but redTE was not found in the non-red-skinned accessions (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig4">4b</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">15</a> and Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM5">1</a>), suggesting that redTE insertion may be responsible for red phenotype in apple. Subsequently, we screened 75 progenies, including 41 with red skin and 34 without red skin, from the cross of Huayue (non-red skin) and Honeycrisp (red skin) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig4">4c</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">16</a> and Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM6">2</a>). The progenies analysis of this cross also confirmed the perfect co-segregation of redTE with red phenotype. These results suggested that redTE insertion was responsible for red phenotype in apple, which further supported the hypothesis of a single dominant mutation model underlying skin colour. Subsequently, because red-fleshed apple usually also have a red skin, we tested whether they also possess redTE insertion. To that end, three red-fleshed accessions with homozygous R6 genotypes were analysed by specific PCR marker, and the results showed that red-fleshed apples didnot harbour this retrotransposon insertion (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">15</a> and Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM5">1</a>), suggesting that the red skin of red-fleshed apples is caused by the constitutive expression of <i>MdMYB10</i> binding to R6 motifs of its own promoter in an auto regulatory-loop manner<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Espley, R. V. et al. Multiple repeats of a promoter segment causes transcription factor autoregulation in red apples. Plant Cell 21, 168â183 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2396">8</a></sup>, independent of redTE.</p><p>Some studies have shown that TEs are an abundant source of enhancer activity in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wei, L. &amp; Cao, X. The effect of transposable elements on phenotypic variation: insights from plants to humans. Sci. China Life Sci. 59, 24â37 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2403">32</a></sup>. Based on its inserted position, redTE appears to provide local enhancer activities that modulate the light-responsive gene expression of the <i>MdMYB1</i>. To definitively assess whether redTE acts as an enhancer, we performed transient assays in <i>Nicotiana benthamiana</i> leaves to test the effects of redTE on the gene expression of firefly luciferase. The results showed that the construct with redTE led to a significant increase in reporter gene expression, relative to the construct with the minimal promoter alone (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig4">4d</a>). This observed enhancement of gene expression by redTE is consistent with the known higher level of <i>MdMYB1</i> expression in red apple, which is similar to the role of a functional transposon (hopscotch) as a long-distance enhancer of <i>tb1</i> gene expression in maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Studer, A., Zhao, Q., Ross-Ibarra, J. &amp; Doebley, J. Identification of a functional transposon insertion in the maize domestication gene tb1. Nat.
