Genet. 43, 1160 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2423">33</a></sup>.</p><p>In addition, many red cultivars originating from bud sports with different red patterns, such as Hanfu and HanM, Gala and GaleGala, etc., are pretty commonly used in apple production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="El-Sharkawy, I., Liang, D. &amp; Xu, K. Transcriptome analysis of an apple (MalusÃdomestica) yellow fruit somatic mutation identifies a gene network module highly associated with anthocyanin and epigenetic regulation. J. Exp. Bot. 66, 7359â7376 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2431">34</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">15</a> and Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM5">1</a>). These cultivars and their bud-sport mutations have redTE in our studies. It is well-known that transposon-induced epigenetic changes often affect the differential expression of neighbouring genes and create novel patterning<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Song, X. &amp; Cao, X. Transposon-mediated epigenetic regulation contributes to phenotypic diversity and environmental adaptation in rice. Curr. Opin. Plant. Biol. 36, 111â118 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2441">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Martin, A. et al. A transposon-induced epigenetic change leads to sex determination in melon. Nature 461, 1135 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2444">36</a></sup>. Additionally, a recent research showed that striped pigmentation of Honeycrisp apple fruits is associated with hyper methylation in the promoter of <i>MdMYB1</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Jaakola, L. New insights into the regulation of anthocyanin biosynthesis in fruits. Trends Plant. Sci. 18, 477â483 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2450">14</a></sup>. Therefore, we selected the fruits of Hanfu (red stripe) and its sports HanM (fully red) at the same time (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">17a</a>), and detected the DNA methylation status of redTE and the promoter region of <i>MdMYB1</i> using McrBC-PCR. The results showed that the Hanfu DNA was methylated in the MR3 and MR7 regions, in agreement with previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Jaakola, L. New insights into the regulation of anthocyanin biosynthesis in fruits. Trends Plant. Sci. 18, 477â483 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2461">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="El-Sharkawy, I., Liang, D. &amp; Xu, K. Transcriptome analysis of an apple (MalusÃdomestica) yellow fruit somatic mutation identifies a gene network module highly associated with anthocyanin and epigenetic regulation. J. Exp. Bot. 66, 7359â7376 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2464">34</a></sup>. The redTE was heavily methylated in the MR8-MR11 regions, while the degree of methylation in Hanfu is much higher than that of HanM in the region MR12 (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">17b</a>), which indicated that redTE-induced epigenetic changes may be associated with the variable colour patterns.</p><p>Given that redTE may control <i>MdMYB1</i> expression in red apple, we aimed to identify regulatory networks of redTE and other transcription factors under different environmental stresses. In blood orange, a retrotransposon controls the fruit-specific accumulation of anthocyanins in response to cold stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Butelli, E. et al. Retrotransposons control fruit-specific, cold-dependent accumulation of anthocyanins in blood oranges. Plant Cell 24, 1242â1255 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2477">30</a></sup>. In apple, a relatively low ambient temperature can also promote fruits coloration, which is presumably regulated via the recruitment of cold acclimation-related transcription factors. In particularly, redTE contains the core cis-acting element (GCCGACTT) for cold acclimation transcription factor DREB/CBF binding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Maruyama, K. et al. Identification of coldâinducible downstream genes of the Arabidopsis DREB1A/CBF3 transcriptional factor using two microarray systems. Plant J. 38, 982â993 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2481">37</a></sup>. We selected the low-temperature-inducible transcription factor MdCBF2 for electrophoretic mobility shift assay (EMSA)-based binding analysis. The result confirmed that MdCBF2 was capable of binding to the GCCGACTT element (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig4">4e</a>), which indicated it is potentially involved in fruit coloration through redTE regulatory networks under a relatively low ambient temperature.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>A high-quality genome assembly is valuable for identifying structural variants, integrating phenotypeâgenotype associations, resulting in insights into the mode and tempo of genome evolution and elucidating the genetic architecture of important traits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Zhang, J. et al. Extensive sequence divergence between the reference genomes of two elite indica rice varieties Zhenshan 97 and Minghui 63. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. 113, E5163âE5171 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2497">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Chakraborty, M. et al. Hidden genetic variation shapes the structure of functional elements in Drosophila. Nat. Genet. 50, 20 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2500">10</a></sup>. The generation of our reference genome provides a high-quality complement to the GDDH13 genome, demonstrating the utility of both whole-genome sequences for the accurate identification of the large and complex SVs, and providing a basis for the comparative genomic investigation of the unique biological characteristics and intraspecific genome diversity in apple. Our comparative genomic results indicate that dynamic changes in TEs can lead to large amounts of SVs, which might impact the genotypes. The discovery of redTE, a locus-specific LTR-RT insertion in the HFTH1 genome, will inspire researchers to further discuss the importance of TEs as a creator of major phenotypic variations. In fact, genome-scale analyses of TE-induced effects are only just beginning to be explored in humans and other crop species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wei, L. &amp; Cao, X. The effect of transposable elements on phenotypic variation: insights from plants to humans. Sci. China Life Sci. 59, 24â37 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2504">32</a></sup>. Therefore, the annotation of TEs in the HFTH1 and GDDH13 genomes will allow detailed studies of the functional effects and dynamic activity of more TEs polymorphisms in the long-term evolutionary background among different apple genotypes.</p><p>The evolution of fruits colour in apple is important and intriguing. In this study, our finding of a redTE insertion upstream of the <i>MdMYB1</i> promoter was associated with red colouration. Thus, redTE was regarded as an enhancer, controlling the development of red colouration by lowering the threshold value of the light response. Cultivars, without this enhancer, fail to effectively produce anthocyanin. This phenomenon could well explain why non-red-skinned cultivars still show a faint red colouration under intense light. In addition, red apple fruits display rich skin colours ranging from stripe to blush to dark red, and these diverse phenotypes are putatively the result of that redTE-mediated control the distribution patterns of anthocyanin through the creation genetic and epigenetic alleles to manipulate the function of <i>MdMYB1</i> under natural conditions. However, in apple, the mechanism through which redTE mediates how genetic and epigenetic variation contributes to red phenotypic diversity and adaptation to light changes remains to be investigated. Furthermore, site-specific TEs can also be used to trace the genetic relationships and origins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Antonius-Klemola, K., Kalendar, R. &amp; Schulman, A. H. TRIM retrotransposons occur in apple and are polymorphic between varieties but not sports. Theor. Appl. Genet. 112, 999â1008 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2517">38</a></sup>. A study of worldwide genetic diversity and pedigree records in apple estimated that one yellow-skinned cultivar Golden Delicious (1916) and four red-skinned cultivars [Coxs Orange Pippin (1850), Red Delicious (1880), Jonathan (1826) and McIntosh (1870)] were the core founders of modern apple breeding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Noiton, D. A. &amp; Alspach, P. A. Founding clones, inbreeding, coancestry, and status number of modern apple cultivars. J. Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci. 121, 773â782 (1996)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2521">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Bannier, H. -J. Modern apple breeding: genetic narrowing and inbreeding tendencies Erwerbs-Obstbau 52, 85â110Â (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2524">40</a></sup>. These four red-skinned cultivars contain redTE insertion (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#Fig4">4b</a>), which suggests that they also had a common parental origin. Notably, the accession <i>M</i>. <i>sieversii</i> also contains redTE, suggesting the redTE likely originated from its supposed primary wild ancestor in Xinjiang (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM5">1</a>). Although, a recent genome resequencing revealed <i>M. sieversii</i> in Xinjiang, China, is an ancient isolated ecotype not directly contributing to apple domestication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Duan, N. et al. Genome re-sequencing reveals the history of apple and supports a two-stage model for fruit enlargement. Nat. Commun. 8, 249 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2544">6</a></sup>, itâs possible that early human activities spreaded <i>M</i>. <i>sieversii</i> with eye-catching red skin, from Xinjiang, China, to other geographical areas. The Chinese soft apple Huacaiping that was domesticated from Xinjiang wild apple with &gt;2000-year cultivation history in China<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Li, Y. An investigation and studies on the origin and evolution of Malus domestica Borkh. in the World. Acta Hortic. Sin. 26, 213â220 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2555">41</a></sup>, harbours redTE, which indicates that red apple once spreaded westward and eastward along the old Silk Road. This result appears to agree well with the results of breeding and geographical investigations of the origins and history of domesticated apple<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Li, Y. An investigation and studies on the origin and evolution of Malus domestica Borkh. in the World. Acta Hortic. Sin. 26, 213â220 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2559">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Harris, S. A., Robinson, J. P. &amp; Juniper, B. E. Genetic clues to the origin of the apple. Trends Genet. 18, 426â430 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2562">42</a></sup>. Subsequently, red apples from the redTE-induced mutation ancestor have become increasingly popular by artificial selection. Interestingly, a recent study of blood oranges<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Butelli, E. et al. Retrotransposons control fruit-specific, cold-dependent accumulation of anthocyanins in blood oranges. Plant Cell 24, 1242â1255 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2566">30</a></sup> showed that two different TE insertions produced similar effects in controlling the cold-induced expression of Ruby, which modulates fruit colour. This finding raises the possibility that other TE insertions are present in apple whose colouring effects have not been characterised. Here, it remains to be seen whether an analogous redTE insertion enhances MYB transcription in pear and other fruits in the Rosaceae.</p><p>Additionally, owing to the long and laborious selection process of apple breeding, the ultimate goal of assembled genome is to serve as a guideline in developing tools for MAS breeding in apple. We identified a surprisingly abundant number of structural variations that were dispersed across the whole-genome, which will be highly useful in developing functional markers for apple breeding. For instance, the redTE-based specific marker, is a particularly valuable tool for pre-selection of hybrid seedlings with objective skin colour in apple breeding programmers, because it is more efficient and precise than the previously available markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Takos, A. M. et al. Light-induced expression of a MYB gene regulates anthocyanin biosynthesis in red apples. Plant Physiol. 142, 1216â1232 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2573">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ban, Y. et al. Isolation and functional analysis of a MYB transcription factor gene that is a key regulator for the development of red coloration in apple skin. Plant Cell Physiol. 48, 958â970 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2576">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="ChagnÃ©, D. et al. A functional genetic marker for apple red skin coloration across different environments. Tree Genet. Genomes 12, 67 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2579">43</a></sup>. The marker may greatly reduce the costs of apple breeding by eliminating a large number of non-target hybrid seedlings. In addition, this genome of HFTH1 from a resistant parent may provide an excellent basis for development of markers of cold-resistance and disease-resistance, such as branch ring-rot and alternaria leaf spot in apple breeding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Zhang, Q. et al. A single-nucleotide polymorphism in the promoter of a hairpin RNA contributes to Alternaria alternata leaf spot resistance in apple (MalusÃdomestica). Plant Cell 30, 1924â1942 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2583">44</a></sup>. Overall, this near-complete genome and other genomic resources will aid the mining of genes and functional markers and support the translation of research findings into genetic improvements for sustainable apple production.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Plant materials and DNA sequencing</h3><p>The homozygous line HFTH1 was derived from an in vitro anther culture of a widely grown cultivar (HFP) in the cold region of northern China. HFTH1 and its donor parent, grafted on GM256 rootstock in 2010, were grown in the greenhouse and field at the Research Institute of Pomology, Chinese Academy of Agricultural Science. The DNA of HFTH1 was extracted from young leaves using the phenol-chloroform method. Two libraries with insert sizes of 300âbp and 20âkb were separately constructed using Illumina TruSeq Nano DNA Library Prep Kits and SMRTbell Template Prep Kits, and the 300âbp and 20âkb libraries were subsequently sequenced using an Illumina HiSeq X Ten instrument and a PacBio RS II instrument with the P6-C4 sequencing reagent, respectively. To obtain the BioNano optical mapping data, DNA was extracted from fresh young leaves of HFTH1, and embedded in a thin agarose layer for labelling at Nt.BspQI sites using the IrysPrep Reagent Kit protocol and subjected to optical scanning on the BioNano Irys platform. The Hi-C library, including cellular crosslinking, chromatin digestion, labelling of DNA ends, DNA ligation, purification and fragmentation, was constructed using the standard procedure as follows. Firstly, nuclear DNA from young leaves was cross-linked in situ, extracted, and digested with a restriction enzyme. The sticky ends of the digested fragments were biotinylated, diluted, and then ligated randomly. The biotinylated DNA fragments were enriched and sheared again to generate sequencing library, which was subsequently sequenced on Illumina HiSeq 4000 (<a href="http://en.annoroad.com/">http://en.annoroad.com/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Genome assembly</h3><p>Falcon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Chin, C. -S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2614">45</a></sup> (v0.4) was used for constructing initial contigs using the following parameters: length_cutoffâ=â13,000 length_cutoff_prâ=â14,000 pa_DBsplit_optionâ=â-x1000 -s250 -a pa_HPCdaligner_optionâ=â-v -dal128 -t12 -e.75 -k20 -h320 -l1800 -s1000 falcon_sense_optionâ=â--output_multi --min_idt 0.75 --min_cov 2 --local_match_count_threshold 2 --max_n_read 400 --output_dformat ovlp_DBsplit_optionâ=â-x1000 -s200 ovlp_HPCdaligner_optionâ=â-v -dal100 -t12 -k18 -h280 -e.96 -l1800 -s1000 overlap_filtering_settingâ=â--max_diff 50 --max_cov 80 --min_cov 2 --bestn 10. The initial polishing was performed with Quiver<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Chin, C. -S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods 10, 563 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2618">46</a></sup> using PacBio-only long reads, and then Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2622">47</a></sup> (v1.20) was utilised to further correct the PacBio-corrected contigs with accurate Illumina short reads. The BioNano data was first assembled to a consensus map using the IrysView software with a molecular length threshold of 150âkb and a minimum labels per molecule of 8, and hybrid scaffolding of the PacBio-corrected contigs and BioNano-based consensus map was performed using the hybrid scaffolding module within IrysView software with manufacturerâs suggested parameters. After scaffolding, PBJelly from PBSuite<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="English, A. C. et al. Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with Pacific Biosciences RS long-read sequencing technology. PLoS ONE 7, e47768 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2626">48</a></sup> (v14.9.9) was performed to close gaps in the hybrid assembly. We re-performed error correction procedures to polish the sequences in the gap regions. Subsequently, the mitochondrial and chloroplast scaffolds or contigs were removed through alignment to mitochondrial and chloroplast references of apple, and any scaffolds or contigs for which at least 80% of the total length was aligned and that showed an identity larger than 90% were discarded as mitochondrial or chloroplast sequences. The Hi-C sequencing data were first aligned to the assembled genome using the bowtie2 end-to-end algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat. Methods 9, 357 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2630">49</a></sup>, then the assembled scaffolds are clustered, ordered and directed onto the pseudo-chromosomes using Lachesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2635">50</a></sup>. Finally, the pseudo-chromosomes predicted by Lachesis were cut into bins with equal length of 100âkb and used to construct a heatmap based on the interaction signals that generated by valid mapped read pairs to do validate and correct manually.</p><p>To obtain the genome of two organelles, we first used BLASR to map all raw PacBio long reads to the organelle references of <i>Malus</i> (downloaded from GenBank, accession NC_018554, NC_031163, KU851961, KX499859 and KX499861), any reads with a length longer than 20âkb, for which at least 80% of the total length were aligned and that showed an identity greater than 70% were used for the next assembly. The chloroplast genome was assembled with Canu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722â736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2645">51</a></sup> (v1.3) (genome sizeâ=â160k) and the mitochondrial genome was assembled with Falcon (v0.4), the following error correction was then performed with the above-described procedures using Quiver and Pilon.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Annotation of repeats</h3><p>The repetitive sequences, including tandem repeats and TEs, in the HFTH1 and GDDH13 genomes were searched. First, we used Tandem Repeats Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucl. Acids Res. 27, 573 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2657">52</a></sup> (TRF, v4.09) to annotate the tandem repeats using the following parameters: 2 7 7 80 10 50 2000. Then TEs were identified at both the DNA and protein levels using a combination of de novo and homology-based approaches. At the DNA level, LTR_FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucl. Acids Res. 35, W265âW268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2661">53</a></sup> (v1.0.6) was first used to identify LTR-RTs and RepeatModeler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="TarailoâGraovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinforma. 4, 4.10 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2665">54</a></sup> (v1.0.5) was utilised to construct a de novo repeat library, which comprised a repeat consensus database with classification information. We employed RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="TarailoâGraovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinforma. 4, 4.10 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2669">54</a></sup> (v4.0.6) to search for similar TEs in the known Repbase TE library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462â467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2673">23</a></sup>, MIPS Repeat Element Database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Nussbaumer, T. et al. MIPS PlantsDB: a database framework for comparative plant genome research. Nucl. Acids Res. 41, D1144âD1151 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2678">55</a></sup> (v9.3) and de novo repeat library. At the protein level, RepeatProteinMask within the RepeatMasker package was used to search against the TE protein database using a WU-BLASTX engine.</p><p>Telomere sequences were identified by searching both ends of the pseudo-chromosomes for high copy number repeats with the repeat unit 5-TTTAGGG-3. Putative heterochromatin regions were identified by searching for 1âMb windows (250âkb step) with &gt;90% repeat elements and the adjacent centromere windows were merged.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>The MAKER pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188â196 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2693">56</a></sup> (v2.31.8), which incorporates ab initio prediction, homology-based prediction and RNA-Seq assisted prediction, was used to annotate gene models. The protein sequences used for homology-based prediction were from five sequenced plants, namely, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (<a href="https://www.arabidopsis.org/index.jsp">https://www.arabidopsis.org/index.jsp</a>)<i>, Prunus persica</i> (<a href="https://www.rosaceae.org/">https://www.rosaceae.org/</a>)<i>, Pyrus communis</i> (<a href="https://www.rosaceae.org/">https://www.rosaceae.org/</a>)<i>, Malus domestica</i> (GDDH13, <a href="https://iris.angers.inra.fr/gddh13/the-apple-genome-downloads.html">https://iris.angers.inra.fr/gddh13/the-apple-genome-downloads.html</a>), and <i>Fragaria vesca</i> (<a href="https://www.rosaceae.org/">https://www.rosaceae.org/</a>) and a total of 1440 benchmarking universal single-copy orthologues of embryophyta within the BUSCO software (v3.0.1), were initially mapped onto the HFTH1 genome using tBlastn. Subsequently, Exonerate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Slater, G. S. C. &amp; Birney, E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinforma. 6, 31 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2749">57</a></sup> (v2.2.0) were used to polish the BLAST hits and thereby acquire exact intron/exon positions. The transcriptional data, including three tissues of the HFTH1 and seven tissues from HFP, were assembled with Histat2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat. Methods 12, 357 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2753">58</a></sup> (v2.05) and StringTie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 290 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2757">59</a></sup> (v1.3.0), and the results were used to identify candidate exon regions, donor and acceptor sites. The repeat regions in the HFTH1 genome were first soft-masked, and MAKER was then run twice. First, we ran MAKER with only transcriptional data to generate imperfect gene models, which were used to train the parameters for SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinforma. 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2761">60</a></sup> (V2006-07-28) and Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucl. Acids Res. 34, W435âW439 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2765">61</a></sup> (v3.2.2). All data and predictions were then used to produce a consensus gene set. We removed a gene model from the consensus gene set if it had a MAKER-defined annotation edit distance (AED) score higher than 0.5 and lacked transcript data or homologous protein support. To obtain a more complete gene set, we added the genes predicted by Augustus that had transcriptional data or homologous proteins support but were not included in the initial gene set, to the final gene set.</p><p>Gene functions were assigned according to the best match by aligning the protein sequences to the Swiss-Prot and TrEMBL databases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Consortium, U. UniProt: a hub for protein information. Nucl. Acids Res. gku989 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2772">62</a></sup> using Blastp (with a threshold of <i>E</i>-valueââ¤â1<i>e</i><sup>â5</sup>). The motifs and domains were annotated using InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236â1240 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2783">63</a></sup> (v5.24) by searching against publicly available databases, including ProDom, PRINTS, Pfam, SMRT, PANTHER and PROSITE. The Gene Ontology (GO) IDs for each gene were assigned according to the corresponding InterPro entry.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">RNA sequencing and data analysis</h3><p>RNA was extracted from seven tissues of HFP and three tissues of HFTH1 using the Quick RNA Isolation Kit (Cat.No.046-50QK, Huayueyang Biotechnology Beijing Co.Ltd., <a href="http://www.huayueyang.com/">http://www.huayueyang.com/</a>) and then characterised by agarose gel electrophoresis and a Nano Drop ND1000 spectrophotometer (Nano Drop Technologies, Wilmington, DE, USA). The complementary DNA (cDNA) libraries were constructed as follows, the first step involves purifying the poly-A containing mRNA molecules using oligo-dT attached magnetic beads. Then, the mRNA is fragmented into small pieces using divalent cations. The cDNA were synthesised using the cleaved RNA fragments as templates. The cDNA fragments then go through an end repair process, the addition of a single A base, and then ligation of the adapters. The products are then purified and enriched with PCR to create the final cDNA library, and are sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 4000. RNA reads were first mapped to the HFTH1 genome using HISAT2, and gene expression was then measured in fragments per kilobase of exon per million fragments mapped (FPKM) using StringTie. The counts of the mapped reads of LTR-RTs were calculated with BEDTOOLS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Quinlan, A. R. &amp; Hall, I. M. BEDTools: a flexible suite of utilities for comparing genomic features. Bioinformatics 26, 841â842 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2803">64</a></sup> (v2.23.0).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>The sequences of protein-coding genes from HFTH1 and seven plants (<i>A. thaliana, F. vesca, R. occidentalis</i> (<a href="https://www.rosaceae.org/">https://www.rosaceae.org/</a>)<i>, P. mume</i> (downloaded from GeneBank, GCA_000346735.1)<i>, P. persica, P. communis</i> and <i>M. domestica</i>) were used for a gene family clustering analysis. First, Blastp was used to generate pairwise protein sequence with an <i>E</i>-value cutoff of 1<i>e</i><sup>â5</sup>. Second, OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2842">65</a></sup> (v2.0.9) was used to cluster genes with an inflation value of 1.5. The protein sequences from 1499 single-copy gene families found in more than eight species were extracted and aligned using MAFFT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Katoh, K. &amp; Standley, D. M. MAFFT multiple sequence alignment software version 7: improvements in performance and usability. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 772â780 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2846">66</a></sup> (v7.058), and the alignment was then back-translated to the nucleotide alphabet using PAL2NAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Suyama, M., Torrents, D. &amp; Bork, P. PAL2NAL: robust conversion of protein sequence alignments into the corresponding codon alignments. Nucl. Acids Res. 34, W609âW612 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2850">67</a></sup> (v14). The poorly aligned positions and divergent regions of the alignment were eliminated using Gblocks (v0.91b, molevol.cmima.csic.es/castresana/Gblocks.html). Phylogenetic analysis was performed using a maximum likelihood (ML) method implemented in RaxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312â1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2855">68</a></sup> (v8.0.19) with the GTRGAMMA substitution model and 100 nonparametric Bootstrap replicates. <i>A. thaliana</i> was selected as the out-group. The divergence time for eight species was estimated based on fourfold degenerate sites from the filtered alignment. The Markov chain Monte Carlo algorithm for Bayes estimation was adopted to estimate the divergence time using MCMCTree within the PAML package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2862">69</a></sup> (v4.6). The calibration time for the divergence between <i>Rosaceae</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> (97~109 Mya) was obtained from the TimeTree database (<a href="http://www.timetree.org/">http://www.timetree.org/</a>).</p><p>SynMap (CoGe, <a href="http://www.genomevolution.org">http://www.genomevolution.org</a>) was used to detect the conserved synteny blocks using homologous gene pairs with the following parameters: Maximum distance between two matches (-<i>D</i>): 20; Minimum number of aligned pairs (-<i>A</i>): 10; Algorithm (Quota Align Merge) with maximum distance between two blocks (-Dm): 500. Circos<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Krzywinski, M. et al. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 19, 1639â1645 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2895">70</a></sup> was used for visualisation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Gap filling</h3><p>A modified protocol based on the method reported by Shi et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Shi, L. et al. Long-read sequencing and de novo assembly of a Chinese genome. Nat. Commun. 7, 12065 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2907">24</a></sup> was used. Briefly, using BEDTOOLS, gap regions were extracted from the GDDH13 genome, and adjacent gaps (distanceââ¤â500âbp) were merged as one gap. The 500âbp fragments located upstream and downstream of each gap region were extracted and aligned to the HFTH1 genome using BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with BurrowsâWheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2911">71</a></sup> with parameter -<i>a</i>. A gap was considered to be closed, if (i) both fragments aligned successfully (coverageââ¥â80%) within 500âkb on the same scaffold or chromosome with the same orientation; (ii) the total alignment positions of both fragments were &lt;5 (both fragments in repeat regions were avoided); (iii) the intervening sequence between aligned positions of both fragments did not contain any ambiguous (N) bases (only consider closed gaps); (iv) the intervening sequences can be aligned successfully (coverageââ¥â80%) with any Golden Delicious genomes or &gt;90% of the intervening sequence with effectively covered (depthâ&gt;3) using 30-fold Illumina reads from GDDH13, the intervening sequences were polished using Pilon with the Illumina reads of GDDH13. The ambiguous (N) bases of gap regions in the GDDH13 genome were replaced with the polished intervening sequences. The gaps in the HFTH1 genome were filled with the GDDH13 genome using the same above-described method.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Detection and analysis of genome variation</h3><p>SNP detection was performed using BWA and Genome Analysis Toolkit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="McKenna, A. et al. The genome analysis toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297â1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2926">72</a></sup> (GATK, v3.8) with the following filtering options: Quality by depth (QD)â&lt; 2.0, Fisher strand (FS)â&gt;â60.0, RMS mapping quality (MQ)â&lt;â40.0, MQRankSumâ&lt;ââ12.5, ReadPosRankSumâ&lt;ââ8.0, Maximum depth (DP)â&gt;â360. An SNP was defined as Golden Delicious shared SNP if the SNP was detected in the Golden Delicious genomes of GDDH13, Velasco et al. and Li et al. but not detected in the HFP genome. Owing to the lack of Illumina reads in the genome reported by Velasco et al., we extracted 500âbp fragments located upstream and downstream of each SNP and aligned these to the genome reported by Velasco et al. to assess whether this SNP was shared. A functional analysis of SNPs was performed using the ANNOVAR software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Wang, K., Li, M. &amp; Hakonarson, H. ANNOVAR: functional annotation of genetic variants from high-throughput sequencing data. Nucl. Acids Res. 38, e164âe164 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2930">73</a></sup>.</p><p>Structural variants (SVs) were identified using BWA and Sniffles<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Sedlazeck, F. J. et al. Accurate detection of complex structural variations using single molecule sequencing. bioRxiv 169557 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2937">74</a></sup> (v1.0.7). First, we mapped the PacBio-corrected long reads (corrected with FALCON during the assembly step) of HFTH1 to the GDDH13 genome using BWA-MEM (using the -<i>M</i> and -<i>x</i> parameters), and Sniffles was then used to identify indels with lengthâ&gt;â100âbp and large inversions with lengthâ&gt;â100âkb. To reduce false-positive SV results as much as possible, we extracted sequences from 500âbp upstream to 500âbp downstream of each indel from the GDDH13 genome and aligned them to the HFTH1 genome using BWA-MEM with the parameter -<i>a</i>; If the fragment was aligned successfully and included a large insertion or clip (corresponding to a deletion of SV), and a large deletion or gap (corresponding to an insertion of SV), this SV was retained in the final result, and the border and length of this SV were recalculated based on the alignment. Long inversions were confirmed by whole-genome alignment using MUMmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2950">75</a></sup> (v3.07).</p><p>Presence/absence variations (PAVs) were detected by whole-genome alignment using MUMmer with the parameter -maxmatch (using the GDDH13 genome as the reference). Using BEDTOOLS, we merge adjacent aligned blocks (distanceââ¤â50âbp) and extracted unaligned regions with lengths longer than 100âbp. For some unaligned regions with ambiguous (N) bases, we removed or split these regions according to the positions of the N bases. These unaligned regions were further filtered using their average depth. First, BWA was used to map the Illumina short reads from GDDH13 and HFTH1 to the HFTH1 and GDDH13 genomes, respectively. The depth was calculated using SAMTOOLS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2957">76</a></sup> (v1.2). If an unaligned region had an average depth &lt;10% of the average depth of the whole-genome, this region was regarded as a PV. The PVs in GDDH13 were treated as deletions, and the PVs in HFTH1 were treated as insertions to ensure consistency with indels for further analysis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Detection and analysis of LTR-RTs</h3><p>The LTR_retriever pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Ou, S. &amp; Jiang, N. LTR_retriever: a highly accurate and sensitive program for identification of long terminal-repeat retrotransposons. Plant Physiol. 01310.2017 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2969">77</a></sup> was conducted to identify intact LTR-RTs of the HFTH1 genome from the outputs of LTRharvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinforma. 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d25851e2973">78</a></sup> (parameters: -similar 90 -vic 10 -seed 20 -seqids yes -minlenltr 100 -maxlenltr 7000 -mintsd 4 -maxtsd 6 -motif TGCA -motifmis 1) and LTR_FINDER (Parameters:-<i>D</i> 15,000 -<i>d</i> 1000 -<i>L</i> 7000 -<i>l</i> 100 -<i>p</i> 20 -<i>M</i> 0.9) with default parameters. Owing to the high copy number of LTR-RTs, we extracted 500âbp fragments in the upstream and downstream of each LTR-RT. We first aligned both fragments back to the HFTH1 genome using BWA-MEM with parameter -<i>a</i>. If both fragments were uniquely aligned within 20âkb (the length of the longest LTR-RT) on the same scaffold and chromosome with the same orientation, this LTR-RT was retained (31 LTR-RTs were removed). We then aligned both fragments to the GDDH13 genome with the same parameters and filter criterion (1482 LTR-RTs were removed), and 5800 LTR-RTs were used for further analysis. The intervening sequences between the aligned positions of both fragments in the GDDH13 genome were aligned to the corresponding LTR-RT sequence in the HFTH1 genome using Blastn with parameter -<i>F</i> <i>F</i>. Different types of LTR-RT were distinguished using the following criteria: if (i) the length of the intervening sequence wasâ&gt;â100âbp and existed blast hits to the corresponding LTR-RT sequence in the HFTH1 genome, this LTR-RT was defined as Shared. The Shared type was further classified as high similarity (similarityââ¥â99%) and low similarity (similarityâ&lt;â99%) with the global similarity; (ii) if two copies of the TSDs flanked the LTR-RT in the HFTH1 genome while only one TSD (no intervening sequences) existed at the corresponding site of the GDDH13 genome, this LTR-RT was defined as Insertion; In contrast, (iii) if the intervening sequence had no blast hits to the corresponding LTR-RT sequence in the HFTH1 genome and two or no TSDs were found at the corresponding site of the GDDH13 genome, this LTR-RT was defined as Elimination; (iv) other remaining LTR-RTs were defined as Unknown.</p><p>The cumulative nucleotide substitution rate (<i>V</i>) of flanking sequences of each LTR-RT was calculated as <i>V</i>â=â(<i>S</i><sub>f</sub>âââ<i>S</i><sub>c</sub>)/<i>T</i><sub>ltr</sub>â+â<i>S</i><sub>c</sub>/(2âÃâ<i>T</i><sub>div</sub>), where <i>S</i><sub>f</sub> is the cumulative nucleotide substitution frequency of flanking sequence, and it is equal to the number of cumulative nucleotide substitutions in the flanking sequence divided by the length of flanking sequence (500âbp); <i>S</i><sub>c</sub> is the cumulative nucleotide substitution frequency of the control region (500âbp) that was defined as a non-functional region (2âkb) with 9âkb away from the LTR-RT; <i>T</i><sub>ltr</sub> is the divergence time of LTR-RT; <i>T</i><sub>div</sub> is the divergence time between Golden Delicious and Hanfu.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Amplification of the partial redTE and 501âbp insertion</h3><p>The two primers (5â²-GGTCACCCAACCCACACTGGGCCTTG-3â² and 5â²-CGGCCGCAATCGCAAGACGCAGA-3â²) were used for amplifying partial sequence of redTE, and the two primers (5â²-GGATACATGCACTATTGATGCGCT-3â² and 5â²-GGGAGTGTGATATCCGACAGTGTGTCT-3â²) were used for amplifying 501âbp deletion sequence. Amplification was performed in a thermal cycler (Bio-Rad, C1000, USA), and the temperature programme consisted of an initial denaturation of 3âmin at 95âÂ°C followed by programmed for 32 cycles of denaturation at 98âÂ°C for 10âs, annealing at 62âÂ°C for 20âs and extension at 72âÂ°C for 30âs, and final extension was for 2âmin at 72âÂ°C. The products were analysed by electrophoresis in 1.5% agarose gel containing ethidium bromide and photographed under a UV transilluminator (Azure C150, USA). The PCR product of 750âbp for redTE and 562âbp for 501âbp insertion were confirmed by Sanger sequencing.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Luciferase reporter assays in <i>Nicotiana benthamiana</i> leaves</h3><p>The two primers (5â²-GGTACCTTATATATGTGTTGACCCTAGAAACTâ3â² and 5â²- GGAAGCTTACGAGCCGAAGCTCAAâ3â²) were used for amplifying redTE, and it was cloned upstream of the 35âS minimal promoter at the <i>Kpn</i>Iâ<i>Hind</i>III sites in pGreenII 0800-LUC vector, generate the reporter construct redTE:minimal 35S:<i>LUC</i>. A reporter construct containing the cauliflower mosaic virus (CaMV) 35S minimal promoter driving expression of the firefly luciferase gene was used to test the control region segments. Two reporter constructs were transformed into <i>Agrobacterium tumefaciens</i> strain GV3101. Bacterial suspensions were infiltrated into young leaves of the 8-week-old <i>N. benthamiana</i> plants using a needleless syringe. After infiltration, plants were grown first under dark for 12âh and then with 16âh light/8âh dark cycle for 60âh at 25âÂ°C. The leaves were sprayed with 100âmM luciferin and maintained under dark condition for 2âmin. The LUC images were captured in a low-light cooled CCD imaging apparatus (Tanon 5200Multi, China). The experiments were repeated independently at least three times with similar results.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">McrBC-based methylation assay</h3><p>A McrBC-PCR method was used to analyse the methylation degree of in the MdMYB1 promoter and redTE regions. Briefly, one microgram of genomic DNA (gDNA) isolated from Hanfu and HanM peel samples were digested overnight with McrBC (New England Biolabs), with three biological replicates. The digested gDNA and its respective control were used as the template for semi-quantitative PCR analysis. The <i>MdMYB1</i> promoter and the redTE sequences were divided into seven and five fragments, respectively, amplified with their corresponding primers (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">12</a>, MR1-MR7 are quoted from reference<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="El-Sharkawy, I., Liang, D. &amp; Xu, K. Transcriptome analysis of an apple (MalusÃdomestica) yellow fruit somatic mutation identifies a gene network module highly associated with anthocyanin and epigenetic regulation. J. Exp. Bot. 66, 7359â7376 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d25851e3102">34</a></sup>) and visualised under the UV Transilluminator (Azure C150, USA) after 1.5% gel electrophoresis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Electrophoretic mobility shift assays</h3><p>The coding region of MdCBF2 (MD06G1072200) was cloned into the pGEX4T-1 vector, and its recombinant vector was transformed into Rosetta (DE3) for induction expression. The electrophoretic mobility shift assays (EMSA) were performed using the LightShift Chemiluminescent EMSA Kit (#89880; Thermo Scientific), according to the manufacturerâs protocol. The unlabelled probes, biotin-labelled probes and biotin-labelled mutant probes at the 3â² end were synthesised by Genewiz Co., Ltd (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM1">13</a>). The protein-DNA samples were separated on 6.5% acrylamide gels, and transferred to a nylon membrane, and signals were captured using ChemiDoc MP Imaging System (BIO-RAD).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Reporting Summary</h3><p>Further information on experimental design is available in theÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM3">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="code-availability" data-title="Code availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="code-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="code-availability">Code availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="code-availability-content">
              
              <p>The repeat annotation and gap filling scripts are available through [<a href="https://github.com/moold/Genome-data-of-Hanfu-apple">https://github.com/moold/Genome-data-of-Hanfu-apple</a>].</p>

              <p>Data generated during the study are deposited in the NCBI under study <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA482033">PRJNA482033</a>. Raw data (PacBio and Illumina reads) have been deposited in the Sequence Read Archive (SRA) under study accession number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRX4557792">SRX4557792</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRX4557793">SRX4557793</a> and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRX4557794">SRX4557794</a>. RNA-seq data of ten tested samples from Hanfu are available under the SRA accession numbers <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRX4557795">SRX4557795</a>-<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRX4557802">SRX4557802</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRX4557790">SRX4557790</a>, and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRX4557791">SRX4557791</a> . Genome assembly and annotation data has been deposited at DDBJ/ENA/GenBank under the accession <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/RDQH00000000.1/">RDQH00000000</a>. The version described in this paper is version RDQH01000000. FASTA files of chromosomes and genes, as well as gff files for gene models can also be downloaded from [<a href="https://github.com/moold/Genome-data-of-Hanfu-apple">https://github.com/moold/Genome-data-of-Hanfu-apple</a>]. Data supporting the findings of this work are available within the paper and its Supplementary Information files. A reporting summary for this Article is available as a Supplementary Information file. The datasets generated and analysed during the current study are available from the corresponding author on reasonable request. The source data underlying Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM7">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM7">3</a>a, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM7">3</a>b, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM7">3</a>c, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM7">3</a>e, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM7">4</a>b and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM7">4d</a>, as well as Supplementary Figs. 3, 6, 11b, 12, 14, 15, 16 and 17 are provided in Source Data file 1. The source data underlying Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM8">3</a>d, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM8">3</a>f, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09518-x#MOESM8">3g</a> are provided in Source Data file 2.</p>
